B008220DGE|3.0|Freddie Quell (Joaquin Phoenix) walks up to and touches a large picture window - \\"I see grass\\" - and then walks to the other side of the room and touches a wood-paneled wall - \\"I see lumber\\". Back and forth. Back and forth. Repeatedly as Lancaster Dodd (Phillip Seymour Hoffman), his wife, Peggy (Amy Adams) and their many followers watch. This is one of Dodd's, aka \\"The Master\\" of a new religious movement called \\"The Cause\\", methods of achieving a 'breakthrough'. But Quell is literally and figuratively hitting his head against the wall.<br /><br />And this is a good analogy for how I felt after watching Paul Thomas Anderson's new film \\"The Master\\". I felt like I was hitting my head against the wall.<br /><br />I am a huge admirer of Anderson's films. \\"Magnolia\\" and \\"There Will Be Blood\\" are two of my favorite films. \\"Punch Drunk Love\\" remains the best and only watchable Adam Sandler film. \\"Boogie Nights\\" is a great film containing some of the best performances of many of the actor's careers.<br /><br />I also admire his films because he is one of a small handful of directors who make films they want to make in the way they want to make them. A three hour-plus look at the intersecting lives of people in the San Fernando Valley? Most studios would laugh in the director's face and slam the door behind them. A film about a turn-of-the-century oil prospector and the first section of the film will have no dialogue? Get out of here. Anderson is one of the few directors who can pull this off. He has only made six films in sixteen years and he tells complex, interesting and unusual stories in exactly the way he envisions.<br /><br />Anderson is one of a handful of directors who seem to have complete control over their films. The film opened a week early in two theaters in Los Angeles and a few theaters in New York. Because he shot the film in 70mm, he requested the film be shown in 70mm and 35mm in addition to the now prevalent Digital Projection. The theaters agreed because they know he goes to such great pains to compose his films, and that their clientele would probably want to see the film in 70mm. But really, how often do you hear of a director doing this? More important, how many studios and theaters would agree to the extra cost? When the film eventually rolls out to theaters across the country, it will inevitably play in shoebox theaters, but it is an indication of the passion of the director that he is willing and able to make such a request. It is a sign of the respect he has earned that such a request is honored.<br /><br />Because he makes each film with such care, and makes so few films, each new story arrives with a lot of anticipation attached. Buzz has been circulating about his newest film \\"The Master\\" almost from the moment it was announced. \\"It is a biography of L. Ron Hubbard, the former science fiction pulp writer who founded Scientology, the home to Tom Cruise, John Travolta, Kirstie Alley and many other Hollywood stars.\\" \\"It is a critique of Scientology, looking at the early days of the movement.\\" \\"It isn't a critique of Scientology.\\" \\"It has nothing to do with Scientology.\\" \\"It isn't about L. Ron Hubbard.\\" And so on.<br /><br />All of these arguments and comments evaporated when Anderson screened the film for Cruise, to get his input and/ or approval. The film does seem like a pretty transparent look at L. Ron Hubbard and the early days of Scientology.<br /><br />But \\"The Master\\" does not reach the same heights as his previous films. And that's a shame, because it has a lot of good things going for it.<br /><br />The production design, the sound design, the performances of the two leads, all great.<br /><br />I would call Anderson an artist, despite this small misstep. He crafts every frame of his films with such exacting detail that you can't help but be transported to the time and place depicted. \\"Master\\" opens with a brief segment depicting Freddie's service in the Navy during the last days of World War II. The scenes show the men trying to stave off boredom and getting into mischief. And they give us a glimpse at the lengths Freddy will go to get buzzed on alcohol. These moments, influenced by Terrence Malick's \\"The Thin Red Line\\", are strangely beautiful in their depiction of the war; they almost seem like they could be pulled from a hyper-realistic revival of \\"South Pacific\\". When the story returns stateside, Freddy gets a job at a department store and you have no doubt the film is set in the 50s. Anderson recreates a grand old store (like an old Bullocks) with such beauty, it makes you feel as though you are actually walking through it. A beautiful yacht, a Manhattan socialite's apartment, a large home, a seedy storefront in Phoenix, each new setting adds to the rich look of the film.<br /><br />Radiohead's Jonny Greenwood composed the music for this film, as he did with \\"There Will Be Blood\\", and it is an unusual score adding a lot of the texture to the film. As Freddie tries to stave off boredom in the Navy, he pounds on a coconut, trying to get it open. These sounds join other bits of ambient noise to become part of the score, creating a music that seems unusual, yet unobtrusive because the sounds are coming from real life. Much like the old Warner Bros. cartoons when the characters would create an 'Anvil Chorus'. Greenwood continues this throughout. It is refreshing in a way, because it is so different from a Hans Zimmer, a Danny Elfman and most definitely very different from a John Williams score. Different in a good way because even my appreciation of Zimmer's and Elfman's work is waning because everything they do sounds so similar.<br /><br />Joaquin Phoenix is almost mercurial as Freddie Quell, the disillusioned serviceman who returns home feeling out of place. Drinking whatever form of alcohol he can get his hands on, he roams from job to job, while losing what little social standing he has, until he wanders onto a yacht being used by Lancaster Dodd for his daughter's wedding. He wakes up the next morning, confronted by Dodd. Apparently, before he passed out, Freddie whipped up a batch of hooch and Dodd is eager for more. Quell seems to find a peace Dodd's words and thoughts and eagerly follows him from stop to stop. In fact, he becomes so enamored of Dodd, he willingly engages anyone who criticizes the new movement in a fist fight.<br /><br />Phoenix's performance shouldn't work, but somehow he pulls it off. Theatrical and over-the-top, Freddie is full of grand gestures and shouts, but Phoenix also embellishes the character with tics and doubt, filtering the grander aspects of his character into something more human. And much more troubled. Freddie Quell is a man who has never lived with much guidance and now that the war is over, he finds himself drifting through life trying to find the next drunken binge. And when he finds Dodd, he also finds one more addiction to feed his lonely life.<br /><br />\\"I am a writer, a doctor, a nuclear physicist, a theoretical philosopher, but above all, I am a man, just like you.\\" There are few actors who could utter this line of dialogue and make us believe it. Thankfully, Philip Seymour Hoffman plays Lancaster Dodd, a former Science Fiction pulp writer who is slowly building a new religion. When he utters this line, you might expect yourself to laugh, but Hoffman is amazingly believable in the role. You believe in his conviction, in his strength, in his disappointment at criticism. He can see this religion so clearly, why can't every one else?<br /><br />When Dodd actually conducts some of the exercises he uses, I couldn't help but stare rapt at the screen. Hoffman's mostly calm, measured performance makes Dodd seem more real and believable. And when he gets upset, these moments simply round out the performance. The character really comes together when Dodd's group visits the apartment of a Manhattan socialite. She has arranged a party, full of wealthy people eager and willing to experience the Cause. Dodd and his entourage arrive and mingle. Later, Dodd begins to lead a woman through \\"Processing\\", the other party guests watching with rapt attention. A man stands to one side watching. As Dodd begins walking the women through the exercise, the man begins to interrupt. But Dodd doesn't acknowledge him and continues. The man interrupts a few times and each time Dodd ignores him. Finally, he can longer be ignored and Dodd angrily turns to him, listening to his criticisms and then answers back. As the story continues, we see Dodd demonstrate different aspects of his new religion and defend it against various attacks. And throughout, Hoffman brings a fine, modulated intensity to the role.<br /><br />If Phoenix is the showier of the two actors, he is perfectly complimented by Hoffman's more restrained, but also volatile performance.<br /><br />Amy Adams is also good as Peggy, Dodd's pregnant wife and most ardent supporter. She quietly stands in the background, always smiling, until she sees the effect Quell is having on Dodd and The Cause. Later, she reveals the level and intensity of her power, making her a suddenly much more interesting character.<br /><br />Even with all of these good things, the film is still missing something, something to tie it all together, to make the film as a whole work.<br /><br />Throughout, we see various degrees of Freddie's erratic behavior. But we never see what he is like in a normal state, or at least, a more normal state. From the beginning, he is bored, restless and on the hunt for the next binge. While it is interesting to watch such an all-consuming portrayal of a man's downward spiral, without some indication of where he started out the journey becomes unanchored and problematic. Freddie doesn't have the roundness of Anderson's other leads. An argument could be made that Freddie's days with the Navy are the beginning, but he is already acting so immature, so at ease, there has to be an earlier beginning which we don't have the opportunity to witness.<br /><br />The reason for Dodd's infatuation with Freddie is also lost on me. From the moment they meet, Dodd seems to feed off something within the drunk, lost soul of his new prot&eacute;g&eacute;. And while Freddie wholeheartedly embraces the ideals of The Cause, he seems to have difficulty actually living by these new ideals. Dodd views his new prot&eacute;g&eacute; as a project, someone he can make better and use as a poster child for his new movement. But Freddie never really improves, so why does Dodd remain infatuated? Peggy even question's her husband's devotion to Freddie.<br /><br />There are a few very brief moments when we begin to get a picture of Dodd's thought processes behind The Cause. But these moments are way too brief and aren't hard hitting or insightful enough to offer any sort of comment, critique or insight into the reasons behind this new religion.<br /><br />And when \\"The Master\\" is done, you still have questions. You still want more. You still need more to connect all the dots.<br /><br />And this has never happened during a Paul Thomas Anderson film before.daughter's wedding. The Wool Series vividly describes the detailed inner workings of a society that has been living in a massive underground survival shelter called a \\"silo\\" for hundreds of years. Every conceivable detail has been considered in the construction of this fictional underground civilization that was created to sustain human life after some unfathomable cataclysmic event. Intricate human relationships develop within the massive 140 floor underground silo that is mostly self-sufficient as though it were some deep space colony. Indeed, the surface of the Earth has been made so inhospitable that it might as well be another planet. The detail and consideration that went into this silo is almost unimaginable. Even the physical features of the silo were designed deliberately to impede communication and cooperation among the residents. The truth is deliberately concealed from the general population as if it was a virus that would destroy the fragile remnants of civilization. So much thought, planning and effort has been made to keep the truth hidden from the general population, supposedly for their own good. Like other great science fiction, Wool explores the idea of how a new discovery might impact the delicately constructed framework of human reality. Hugh Howey deserves a great deal of credit for the thought and imagination that he poured into the incredibly intelligent story that is destined to become a science fiction classic. Remarkable in its breadth and scope, it is certain that Wool will leave a lasting impression in your mind.

B008C1O8CA|3.0|First things first: yes this is better than \\"21st Century Breakdown\\". I have already listened to this twice, which is one more time than I bothered spinning that record.<br />Secondly, there is no overarching theme here, no grander story they are trying to tell. And thank God for that. \\"Uno!\\" is a straight up pop-punk record with twelve catchy tunes that borrow greatly from other artists but still sound like Green Day. You can even periodically hear Mike Dirnt's bass (always my favorite part of Green Day) underneath the multitudes of guitars.<br />So why the three song rating? Well, first off, there's a lot of inflation of ratings in this site. People want to give anything they enjoyed five stars. Well, that to me means classic. \\"London Calling\\", \\"Exile on Main Street\\", \\"The Queen is Dead\\". And this is not any of those records. But also, simply put, this record, while fun to listen to, never seems to have any passion to it.<br />Really it seems like these songs were written and recorded as an excuse to go out on tour. And while they may sound good during your next Green Day show, I can't see anyone six months from now really wanting to listen to \\"Carpe Diem\\" or \\"Troublemaker\\", good songs they may be, over anything they released over their lengthy career. Add to that, there are a few tracks here that simply did not work for me. \\"Kill The DJ\\" is fun enough but really just made me want to pull of my Franz Ferdinand record rather than listen to it again. \\"Sweet 16\\" struck me as sort of creepy and weird being sung by men in their forties. And the lead off single \\"Oh Love\\" still sounds like the most mediocre Who song ever written.<br />So, should you buy Green Day \\"Uno!\\" Well, that really depends. If you are a longtime fan, why not? You will get a nostalgic blast from the record and it's always fun to know the new songs when you are going to see a band live. You probably will file it away in a few months and not return to it often, but I'm sure it will look cool with yourCD collection or in your iTunes library.<br />New to the band, however? Probably not your best to start. Because while \\"Uno!\\" is a perfectly pleasant album its never a great one. And Green Day have released a few of those in their career.

Finally, I decided on 4 stars, as the book was griping and surprising at times--a quality that I did not think the first two books possessed.<br /><br />*Spoiler alert*<br /><br />For the first 1/3 or 1/4 of the book, I found myself utterly disappointed.  I expected some sort of grand unveiling of District 13.  Instead, I found myself utterly plunged into the new story without much time to even catch my breath.  Throughout this first part of the book, we find that the two runaways that Katniss met in Catching Fire never made it to District 13, but no explanation is given, other than speculation that they must have died along the way, which I find somewhat difficult to believe.  After getting only a little ways into it, I actually considered stopping reading it.  The only reason I read the second book was to find out what happened, and in the end, I felt utterly disappointed.  I did not want to feel the same way after finishing this book.  Looking back, I'm glad I finished it.<br /><br />The remaining parts of the book were griping enough.  Peeta's return was surprising and I actually felt sad for Katniss when I discovered that he was brainwashed.  For me, the end of the war was satisfying, but in the sense that it was realistic, which was something I did not expect.  For me, what made this book so great were the ideas behind it.  Katniss being used as a pawn, propaganda being so powerful in the war.  Both sides used tools to try and sway the public as it were.  Using Prim's death to manipulate Katniss actually made me stop for a second to think.  It was definitely a well-thought out plot point by Suzanne Collins.<br /><br />In the end, the reason I was so torn about giving the book 3 or 4 stars was because some of the biggest arcs in the book left me feeling cheated and unsatisfied.  When Peeta returned to Katniss, I couldn't feel happiness, I could only feel sadness.  In the end, it was almost Peeta was chosen over Gale for her; she did not choose Peeta herself.  The ending of the book also left me feeling that way.  It was almost as if Katniss never found happiness.  Although she got a happy ending, I think she was still very sad on the inside.  Perhaps it is more realistic in that sense, but I honestly wanted to feel satisfied at some point in Mockingjay, because I followed Katniss from the first book.  I felt that my dedication to the heroine was not rewarded in any way.  At the very least, I think Katniss should have had some sort of character growth.  She spent all three books being manipulated and the final puppet string to control her was losing Prim.  Instead of living the rest of her life in misery, she should have severed that string.  While, I think it is perfectly acceptable for her to have grieved for Prim for a while, I think I really wanted to see the girl that fought for her life in the Hunger Games pick herself up and move forward instead of staring back at the past.

0062082353|4.0|U.S. Navy SEAL sniper Chris Kyle has more than 160 confirmed kills -- 255 if you cut out all the ridiculous paperwork one has to fill out to get credit for a single confirmed kill. Add in politicians insisting on nonsensical Rules of Engagement (an insurgent can get away with killing a U.S. troop by dropping his gun and giving up) and you get a sense of the sanitized war men like Kyle had to fight in order to avenge 9/11 and protect his country and fellow SEALs and U.S. Marines.<br />This super fast-reading book is the story about a good ole boy from Texas who wanted to challenge himself as far as he could by becoming a Navy SEAL. When the Twin Towers came down, Kyle, whom his teammates jokingly called \\"The Legend,\\" went on to show how much damage one man could do during four deployments in Iraq, which he describes as a \\"target-rich environment.\\"<br />Though he claims not to be a scorekeeper, Kyle went on to record more kills than any other U.S. sniper in history, including the legendary Gunnery Sergeant Carlos Hathcock (USMC). Did it bother his Christian morals? Not when he sees the enemy as evil \\"savages,\\" which is hard to argue with given the nature of their heinous acts.<br />The book is stuffed with some vivid details of Kyle's best shots -- a two-in-one on a couple of moped-riding, RPG-carrying mujahideen (\\"I mean, come on,\\" writes Kyle), a record 2100-yard shot, and his own unique tale of taking down attackers floating toward him on beach balls.<br />It's these types of stories, not to mention acts of valor (Kyle earned two Silver Stars, five Bronze Stars with a 'V' for Valor, and three other medals and one commendation) that make the book so easy to read and almost -- but not quite -- cause the reader to wonder if he or she is feeling a guilty pleasure.<br />For his part, Kyle offers no apologies and would have been happy to add to his record, which includes taking out a woman who pulled the pin on a Chinese grenade while she had her child in tow.<br />It issort of a different autobiography, one in which his wife offers her feelings and thoughts on Kyle. I didn't mind the injection of another point of view, especially when the divorce rate among SEALs is 95-percent.<br />Kyle takes you through his days in the rodeo and BUD/S training, not to mention his breakdown on his many instruments of death -- his rifles. Kyle not only was a sniper, but kicked in many doors in support of the Marines during their battle in Fallujah. There are many heart-wrenching moments, as it wasn't only insurgents who were doing the dying.<br />Also of note is Kyle's story of punching out a famous ex-SEAL whom he doesn't name, but has been rumored to be Jesse Ventura.<br />Raucous, rowdy, record-setting -- Kyle's story is more than interesting and unique enough for you to buy the book and judge for yourself.

Gillian Flynn just became my ultimate literary crush. As a writer of contemporary fiction, I have a true appreciation for the mastery of writing. Ms. Flynn's command of language, narrative, and dialog is a talent to behold. I first read the Kindle version of \\"Gone Girl\\"; and then I bought the hardcover copy so I could feed my incessant need to study her talent and better understand her literary technique and style. Flynn's ability to bring out both the worst and best in her characters is easily splayed across each and every page. No notion is left unexplored or unattended as Flynn effectively dismantles the romanticism that still persists around the quest for the perfect marriage, the perfect relationship, and in being the perfect beautiful couple for the world. Flynn toys with readers at the beginning and in her own fastidious way lets the story out in infinitesimal detail in the very first paragraph on page one:<br /><br />\\"What are you thinking, Amy? The question I've asked most often during our marriage, if not out loud, if not to the person who could answer. I suppose these questions stormcloud over every marriage: What are you thinking? How are you feeling? Who are you? What have we done to each other? What will we do?\\"<br /><br />Just the choice use of the verb stormcloud bowls me over. Wow.<br /><br />Readers slowly discern that handsome Nick Dunne is not all that he portrays himself to be and that his narcissism is not all that far removed from his reality, nor is he all that sorry about it.<br /><br />The unraveling begins. And, it takes a while for readers to realize it is \\"us\\".<br /><br />\\"Okay,\\" we say, even as we start to guess what's gone wrong here. But, do we really know?<br /><br />And, just when we think we've figured it out, the story gets more sinister as Flynn subtly reminds all of us that this roller coaster ride is far from over. And,as she finesses further with growing intrigue, readers are introduced to Amy through a variety of diary entries. Invariably, readers will buy into all of this clever character's thoughts and feelings--hook, line and sinker. We're all but drowning in the psychotic push-pull of the back and forth narrative of these two, even while we sense the unraveling that exudes so deliberately from both Amy and Nick.<br /><br />Invariably, as readers, we will want to save the victims. But, which ones are they? Who do we save? Ourselves? Flynn flexes her literary brilliance throughout this novel, both in her ability to write and tell the story, but also in the subtle way she keeps all of us captivated by it...<br /><br /><more> at: bit(dot)ly/GRsReviewGoneGirl

0547564651|4.0|If your grandparent said to you \\"character counts\\", you'd probably just think that they're just passing on to you an age old cliche.  The author Paul Tough has written a book based almost exclusively on this theme.  But the odd thing is that in this day and age where a parent is in competition with video games, television, videos, and the list of distractions seems almost endless, it's easy to forget that grit, determination, and conscientiousness are key ingredients to the success of a great number of immigrants that entered this country starting almost back to the Declaration of Independence.  So, the novelty here is that it's linked to long term educational success.  Is this a surprise, I hope not.  Is it comforting that research outlined by Paul Tough in the field of psychology, child development, and education show that putting problem children on Prozac isn't the answer, I hope so.<br /><br />If all of this intrigues you, and perhaps you need a little boost on the important things in not only raising your own child, but also develop some understanding of possibilities to help the disadvantaged students, then this is a great book to read.  I found the first two chapters captivating, interesting, and thinking to myself how far off the rails the American culture has gotten with all the distractions for not only ourselves, but also our children.  But for poor children, the distractions are real violence and real home instability, something that is highlighted in this book.  The author provides examples of some pretty great people who have taken on the responsibility of building a future for children that left alone would have no hope of escaping their poverty.<br /><br />Why 4 stars?<br />The book started incredibly strong and slowly faded in substance and framework, and finally the last chapter almost seemed stream of conscious.  The chapter entitled \\"How To Think\\" was almost exclusively about chess and education.  If you read through my reviews you'll see that I'm a chess player.  I even played chess for a school mentioned in the book (back in the day!), but I found his development of the chapter not only without any real framework, but he really avoided the question that he raised in the title of the chapter, How to Think?  The United States Chess Federation sent out an email to all its members and highlighted the book as an example of the importance of chess in education, but I'm not so sure that even the slightest critical review would be convincing that it taught anyone to think.  So, I need to be clear, chess DOES help children develop critical thinking skills.  It teaches them to think both linearly (concretely) and nonlinearly (abstractly).  The real problem in the book is that the example of Elizabeth Vicary and her students is an excellent example of grit and determination (again, the first chapter) and not so much how to think.  He hits rock bottom when he gives examples of Gata Kamsky, many times US chess champion, dropping out of medical school and failing the bar exam!  What was he thinking in adding this example?  Susan Polgar is yet another example of the chess prodigy force fed chess by a father doing a social experiment.  And how is this relevant to normal child development and ways to teach critical thinking skills?  It's simply a swing and a miss by the author who was caught up in the feel good story of the Brooklyn public school that went to battle the polished, well-funded high schools and came away with the trophies.  And for the record, it's a wonderful story despite the fact that the author felt the need to delve into the personal affairs of Elizabeth Vicary who is the heroine of the chapter, but info on her engagement and subsequent marriage is completely superfluous to how children learn to think playing chess.  The real highlight of the chapter was related to some almost randomly placed quotes from the Scottish GM Rowson.  Hopefully, WHEN you read this book, and you should, you'll simply walk away from the section on chess and mentally note that having your child play chess will help them develop critical thinking skills.  And if you're really industrious, you'll find the many POSITIVE examples of how youth and chess have lead to great careers including Harvard economists, university professors, high-caliber software engineers and the list goes on and on.<br /><br />In summary, the importance of character is truly forgotten not only in education, but also in the workplace.  Grit, determination, and other skills to fight the many modern battles with adversity is more important than it ever was in the past.  As opportunities diminish (with a growing world economy), we need our children to have the grit and determination to develop great study habits leading to great work habits and as the author points out, puts them in a position to succeed in the future.<br /><br />This book is for just about anyone, period.  You might not be impressed with it from cover to cover, but it does carry an important and timeless message.  I hope that the message in this book is the start of a very positive trend and by reading this book, you'll find your place in either agreeing with it, or perhaps improving on the ideas.

Battle Born is completely devoid of filler.<br /><br />\\"Flesh And Bone\\" opens Battle Born superbly, firmly invested in neo- New wave sensibility.  Brandon Flowers sounds phenomenally polished and delivers the  lofty subject matter of the  first verse capably (\\"...This natural selection picked me out to be/a dark horse running in a fantasy...\\"). Flowers sells the chorus flawlessly: \\"...Am I running out of time? Follow-up \\"Deadlines and Commitments,\\" finds Brandon Flowers honing in on his falsetto, contrasting grittier vocals from earlier.  Superb songwriting and a solid, buttressing rhythmic groove makes \\"Deadlines and Commitments\\" as consistent and everything else.<br /><br />\\"Miss Atomic Bomb,\\"  produced by Stuart Price, yields a sound similar to \\"Human\\" though by no means copy-cat. \\"Miss Atomic Bomb\\" is less accomplished than its comparison cut, but not by much.  \\"The Rising Tide\\" keeps the production `in-house'. It continues to build a arsenal solid `role playing' songs, even if it fails to top the best.  Plenty of positives reside here including the dirty timbre of the electric guitar solo, lyrical prowess, and the harmonic underpinnings.<br /><br />\\"Heart of A Girl\\" adds the Las Vegas Master Singers on background vocals.  Initially restrained, the cut paces its way to be more overt and intense. \\"Heart Of A Girl\\" is classified as `second tier,' only because of stiff competition on this affair.<br /><br />\\"From Here On Out\\" delivers a KO punch in a brief 2:30 duration.  The tempo is quick while  New Wave is supplanted with country-rock.  Flowers cycles agilely through the lyrics of the quick-paced chorus: \\"Hey, from here on out/Friends are going to be hard to come by/left to wonder what it all was about/he had it easy then he chose the hard way...\\" \\"From Here On Out\\" is stellar.<br /><br />\\"Be Still\\" follows-up strongly, highlighted through its blend of real, acoustic and synthetic instruments flawlessly.  Closer \\"Battle Born\\" doesn't disappoint, featuring keyboards/programming (Damian Taylor), violins, and background vocals (Las Vegas Master Singers). B008NW67E0|4.0|Mumford & Sons 2012 effort [[ASIN:B008NW67E0 Babel]] is easily one of the year's most anticipated efforts. A solid album overall, first time Mumford & Sons fan may enjoy Babel `better' than the most invested fans; more invested fans may pinpoint Babel as `formulaic' and `safe' rather than innovative.  Arguments presented, Babel is sure-fire commercial hit that may not necessarily `progress' the band's artistry to `the next level' as expected. A superb group of musicians, as good as Babel is, it may be ever so slightly humbler than the hype.<br /><br />Opener \\"Babel\\" is characterized by Mumford & Sons's `rhythmic machine' (acoustic guitar, banjo) and set in six-eight time.  Ben Lovett's piano provides a chordal foundation, preempting  contrast from Marcus Mumford's gritty vocals in favor of smoother, more melodic-centered vocals.  \\"Babel\\" is ultimately well conceived; it ends with cliffhanging airy pad segueing into \\"Whispers in the Dark.\\"<br /><br />\\"Whispers in the Dark\\" possesses constant rhythmic motion throughout the verses, though it is softer allowing for Marcus's vocals to shine without strain.  `Wall of sound' vocals aid in giving the record an inviting timbre, committed to folk. \\"Whispers\\" is less emotionally dynamic than \\"Babel\\" however.<br /><br />\\"I Will Wait\\" receives valedictory honors easily. The biggest quibbles personally are that the band can/should take more risks instead of clinging to safety and `formulaic' means. B001KVZ6HK|4.0|When Thor's mischievous brother, Loki, makes a deal with the alien race Chitauri to help them secure the Tesseract Cube so they can conquer the galaxy, the Earth suddenly falls into great peril. With even the powerful top secret agency S.H.I.E.L.D. having difficulty containing Loki, there is only one call S.H.I.E.L.D. Director Nick Fury can make: Avengers Assemble!<br /><br />The team is gathered--Captain America, Iron Man, Thor, Hulk, Black Widow, Hawkeye--set out to do battle with Loki and his alien cohorts. If they don't overcome their differences and learn to work together as a team, the Earth will fall and Loki will rule the planet.<br /><br />The Avengers brings together Earth's mightiest heroes to combat a force of evil so great they either stand together or fall together, with the fate of the planet--even the galaxy--hanging in the balance.<br /><br />The Avengers is a difficult movie to review, more so, give a proper rating to because this movie is very much black and white between its story and its presentation, so that said, I'm going to quickly go over both and you'll see where I'm coming from at the end.<br /><br />The story: This is a single-plot movie, very much an A-to-B narrative and incredibly simple--too simple. Aliens are coming, we need to stop them so we'll get the Avengers to do it. That's it. From a storytelling perspective, it's too simple and too predictable. Big bad guy, big good guy(s), let's fight, good guys win. The end.<br /><br />However, if you view The Avengers as an end cap/final act to all the movies leading up to it: Iron Man, Incredible Hulk, Iron Man II, Thor, Captain America--then you have something that definitely serves its purpose and more or less let's each character shine for the same amount of time. In this case, a simple story works despite, um, the many continuity flaws from the previous movies (i.e. Thor somehow now able to come to Earth, which renders the ending of Thor's movie moot, Tony Stark called upon to be Iron Man in the Avengers despite being banned from doing so in Iron Man II, the characters communicating to each other without earpieces or any communication devices. Maybe they're telepathic?).<br /><br />The presentation: This movie is a nerd's dream come true from start to finish. Assemble your favorite superheroes--of which each were spotlighted in their own movies, almost--put them together and have them go toe-to-toe with a larger-than-life threat that will squash the planet if they don't come through.<br /><br />From an eye candy perspective, this movie nailed it. Huge battles, lots of explosions, combat action, hammer throwing, Hulk smashing, shield boomeranging, repulsors firing, arrows shooting, girl fists punching--yeah, it has it all.<br /><br />It's also very important to point out that the casting of Mark Ruffalo as Bruce Banner/Hulk was an amazing choice. I honestly wasn't too thrilled with the news when I first heard it, with Ruffalo being more a chick-flick romance guy, but he got the role done so well that if there's a spin-off, I hope he gets the job. He's definitely earned it.<br /><br />Chris Evans as Captain America--a Superman performance, which is good and brought a traditional superhero element to the team. As the running joke was throughout the movie, a little \\"old-fashioned\\" was what was needed.<br /><br />Robert Downey Jr. as Iron Man--do I really need to talk about this? He's the same Tony Stark from the first two movies, the only difference being he's mellowed out a bit because, despite his arrogance, he understands life isn't all about him and there's other people out there, too. This bit really comes through in this movie.<br /><br />Chris Helmsworth as Thor--bold, poetic, commanding, everything his character is supposed to be so kudos to him for carrying on with a great performance from the stand-alone movie.<br /><br />Jeremy Renner as Hawkeye--I don't know much about the comic character other than he's like Green Arrow, but perhaps with a more military-mind-set, so I can't comment. Renner did sell me on Hawkeye though, but why couldn't they give him that awesome mask? Maybe in the sequel.<br /><br />Scarlett Johansson as Black Widow--she really comes into her own in this flick because in Iron Man II, it was more a back-up appearance so we didn't know much about her. I'm glad she got the screen time she deserved and, come on, her fight scenes were fantastic.<br /><br />Tom Hiddleston as Loki--he's the bad guy you love to hate, the one that, even just looking at him, you want to punch in the face. If we just go back to his last albums (i.e. 4x4=12, which is my favorite album), you see that there are songs as Cthulhu Sleeps, which at 11:00 and using a repetitive sound progression is prone to annoy the masses, rather than entertain them.<br /><br />This new album I bought kind of scared. For Lack of a Better Name has both Ghosts and the masterpiece Strobe, but the rest of the songs are subpar. Still, I decided to buy it. What a pleasant surprise.<br /><br />Superliminal is a heavy track. Fantastic, though a little repetitive.<br />Channel 42 is a highlight, but with Wolfgang Gartner it was foolish not to expect it, especially after Animal Rights. It's easily one of the best songs in the album, its upbeat and happy and has some nice drops, without being brutal.<br />The Veldt is fantastic as I already knew thanks to Youtube. Reminisces his slow tunes like the beginnings of Strobe and  Raise Your Weapon, but with vocals. The mix is very balanced and the vocals help those not into repetitive riffs.<br />Fn Pig as also there, a fantastic 8+ minute majestic piece with all the mood changes you could expect from a song. The buildup and the drops are orchestrated fantastically. This is probably the best song in the album.<br />Professional Griefers, wow. That has some shoes  to fill, as the non vocal version was released a bit ago, and it made my car's side mirrors vibrate with the bass. One of my favorite overall mau5ongs, I feared Gerard would destroy it. Quite opposite, I find myself humming the song more and remembering the lyrics. It was a great choice, but I'll always cherish the original better.<br />Maths was another one released a bit ago. It is very happy as well, bouncy if you will. It's good and catchy.<br />There Might Be Coffee is fantastic. Great song development and the song is heavy but cheery. Another one of the best songs.<br />Take Care Of The Proper Paperwork is messy. It makes up for it near the middle, but it's definitely not noteworthy.<br />Closer is another great one. A slow, dreamy song with a cathcy high pitch riff, like a mix of Faxing Berlin with Slip, its a progressive jewel.<br />October is a song that builds up through most of its duration and never really explodes, which is kind of sad, but the atmospheric beat is very good.<br /><br />Unfortunately, only the first 10 songs are good. The last three are very poor, specifically Failbait. I guess you could enjoy Miscommunications, but I certainly didn't.<br /><br />Long Story Short:<br /><br />If you like deadmau5' work as full albums, this is definitely for you. The production is masterful and it's certainly the modern Joel you'll be listening to, no surprises there. If you just know a couple of songs, maybe use youtube to find out how his non singles sound like. If you enjoy songs like Faxing Berlin, Cthulhu Sleeps, Aural Psynapse and HR 8938 Cephei, you might consider buying this album.
